I snatched the vessel from the flame, my fingers wrapped in three layers of cloth, and tipped the liquid toward the catch-basin. One drop escaped, touched the wooden table, and the wood puckered. Not burned\u2014flinched. The drop shivered, gathered itself, and shot upward, out the open window, leaving behind a faint trail of ozone and the smell of something that had been very, very afraid.\n\nI had spent six months acquiring that tincture. Six months traveling to the outer markets, bartering my grandmother's silver combs and three bolts of Thronegate silk. The Ythrian merchant with oil-slick eyes had sworn it could pull trauma from the marrow like a splinter from a thumb. Now the tincture had just... fled. I stared at the empty catch-basin, and for a moment I considered screaming, but I had stopped screaming in Year 31.\n\n\"Rosa.\" Rogr's voice from the bedroom. Thin. Even. The voice of a man who had forgotten how to inflect.\n\n\"Coming,\" I called, and I manufactured brightness the way a factory manufactures sheet metal. I had learned to make my voice bright. It was a survival mechanism, like the way your hand learns to pull back from a hot iron, except the hot iron was my husband's silence and I could not pull away from it.\n\nI found him in the armchair by the window, the one that caught the afternoon light. He sat in the light but did not seem to feel it. His hands lay on his thighs, palms up, fingers slightly curled. Ten years ago, those hands had been a carpenter's hands\u2014calloused, sure, always moving. Now they were still. They were always still.\n\nI knelt beside him and took one of those hands. The skin was cool and dry. \"I made you some tea,\" I said. \"The blend from the eastern provinces. You liked it last winter.\"\n\n\"That's nice,\" he said, and his eyes did not move from the window. Outside, the spires of Thronegate City glittered in the midday sun. The king's banner, silver on black, snapped in a wind I could not feel.\n\n\"I saw the banners are up,\" I said, because silence with Rogr was a living thing that grew if you let it. \"There's a parade next week. For the twentieth year of the Awakening.\" I paused. \"Pete wants to go. He's been talking about it for a month.\"\n\nAt the mention of our son's name, something moved in Rogr's face. Not a smile. A rearrangement of shadows. \"He should go,\" Rogr said.\n\n\"We should all go,\" I said. \"As a family.\"\n\nRogr's hand twitched in mine. A spasm so brief I might have imagined it. \"I don't... crowds are...\" He stopped. He often stopped mid-sentence, as if the words had been stolen from his mouth by a thief only he could see.\n\n\"I know,\" I said. \"I know, love. It's all right.\"\n\nBut it was not all right. It had not been all right for ten years.\n\n---\n\nI will continue writing now, but let me pause and check the structure. I've established the present moment, Rosa's voice, Rogr's condition. The delegation had been a great honor\u2014newly subjugated universes sending representatives to pledge fealty to the king. Rogr had been selected as a cultural liaison, a role that recognized his years of service in the merchant guild. He had left our home on a Tuesday morning, the spring light catching the gray at his temples, and he had kissed me on the forehead and told me he would be home for supper.\n\nHe was not home for supper. He came home three days later, his clothes unchanged, his face a ruin of something I could not name.\n\n\"What happened?\" I had asked, pulling him inside, my hands checking him for wounds. There were no wounds. There was no blood. There was only the look in his eyes, which was the look of a man who had seen something that did not fit inside a human skull.\n\n\"Nothing,\" he had said. \"Nothing happened. It was... the king. I met the king.\"\n\nThe king. Godric the Eternal. The benevolent ruler who had given us the Awakening, who had made Thronegate the center of the omniverse, who ensured that no woman walked in fear and no child went hungry. The king whose portrait hung in every public square, whose voice on the broadcasts was warm and paternal. The king I loved.\n\n\"What about the king?\" I had pressed.\n\nRogr had opened his mouth. And then he had closed it. And then he had started to shake\u2014a fine, rapid trembling that began in his fingers and spread inward, until his entire body was quaking like a plucked string. I had wrapped him in blankets and held him until the shaking stopped, and when it stopped, something in him had stopped with it.\n\n---\n\nGood. Now I need to show her reverence for Godric. This should be genuine and heartfelt:\n\n---\n\nI did not understand. The king was good. Everyone knew the king was good. Under his rule, Thronegate had become a paradise\u2014streets clean of crime, markets overflowing with goods from a thousand universes, children growing up with strong bones and straight teeth. The Awakening had made our men mighty, and that might was used only to protect, never to oppress. When I walked through the city, I saw women smiling. I saw old men playing chess in the parks. I saw young couples holding hands without fear. This was the world Godric had built, and I was grateful for it with every fiber of my being.\n\n---\n\nNow Pete's idolization:\n\n---\n\nMy son Pete adored the king with the uncomplicated fervor of the young. He collected the commemorative coins minted for each year of Godric's reign. He could recite the names of the five Thronegate men who had conquered Elaris as easily as other boys recited sports statistics. When he spoke of the king, his voice grew hushed and reverent, as if he were speaking of a household god who happened to sit on a throne.\n\n\"Father met him,\" Pete would say to his friends, his chest puffing with pride. \"Father was in the delegation. He stood in the same room as the king.\"\n\nAnd Rogr, whenever he overheard this, would turn his face to the wall and say nothing.\n\n---\n\nNow I need to show Rosa's elaborate attempts. Let me include 2-3. I'll show them failing in increasingly horrifying ways:\n\n---\n\n**Attempt 1 (Year 30 - two years after):** A memory-weaver from the Hathorian Collective. She arrived in robes of spun silver, her fingers long and delicate as spider legs. She claimed she could enter a patient's memories and smooth the jagged edges of traumatic recollection. Rogr agreed, because he still agreed to things back then.\n\nShe placed her fingertips on his temples, closed her eyes, and began to hum. The hum was a soothing thing, a lullaby from a distant star. And then she screamed. The sound was not a sound so much as a physical extrusion\u2014a spike of pure, undiluted terror that shattered every glass object in our home. She wrenched her hands away from Rogr's head, and I saw that her fingertips were blackened, as if she had touched a live wire. She fled our house without speaking, and the next day we heard she had booked passage to the farthest edge of the known omniverse and refused to ever speak of what she had seen.\n\nRogr apologized to me. He apologized, as if it were his fault.\n\n**Attempt 2 (Year 33):** A chrono-therapist who could unwind time around a specific memory, diluting its impact by spreading it across multiple timelines. He set up his equipment in our living room\u2014a series of crystalline rods that hummed with temporal energy. He calibrated the rods, aimed them at Rogr, and activated the device.\n\nThe rods melted. Not from heat\u2014there was no heat. They melted from sheer ontological rejection, as if the very concept of their function had been declared invalid by some higher authority. The therapist stared at the slag dripping onto my floor and said, very quietly, \"There is nothing wrong with your husband that I can fix.\" He packed his surviving equipment and left without charging a fee.\n\n**Attempt 3 (Year 36):** A dream-catcher woven from the silk of chrono-spiders, creatures that spun webs across the axis of time itself. It was supposed to filter nightmares, to catch the dark thoughts before they could sink their hooks into the sleeping mind. I hung it above Rogr's bed with my own hands, and for one night, it held.\n\nIn the morning, the dream-catcher had unraveled. The threads had recoiled into a tight, terrified knot no larger than a walnut. When I touched it, the knot emitted a sound\u2014a high, keening whimper that went on and on until I buried it in the garden under three feet of soil. I could hear it for days afterward, a muffled, hopeless cry rising through the earth.\n\n---\n\nThese are good. They show the \"self-destruct in fear\" aspect. Now I need to build to Blake's visit.\n\nRosa's despair is growing. Rogr is getting worse. She's running out of options. And then...\n\n---\n\nBy Year 38, I had exhausted every remedy my mind could conceive and every resource my hands could gather. Rogr had stopped leaving the bedroom. He ate when I brought him food, but he no longer seemed to taste it. His carpenter's hands, once so strong, had grown thin and translucent. I could see the veins beneath his skin, blue rivers mapping a country I could not visit.\n\nPete had stopped bringing friends over. He still loved the king\u2014that love was too deeply rooted to be dislodged\u2014but he had learned not to speak of the delegation in front of his father. He had learned to lower his voice, to move carefully, to live in a house that had become a sickroom.\n\nI was in the kitchen washing dishes on a Thursday afternoon when the knock came at the door. Not a servant's knock. Not a neighbor's. This knock was firm, deliberate, and accompanied by a presence that made the air in the hallway thicken.\n\nI opened the door. The man standing on my threshold was old\u2014seventy-four, I would later learn\u2014but his shoulders were straight and his eyes were sharp. He had a friendly mutton chop, the kind of facial hair that made a man look like someone's favorite uncle. He wore the robes of a court wizard, and behind him, the street was strangely empty, as if the city itself had decided to give this conversation privacy.\n\n\"Rosa,\" he said, and his voice was heavy. \"My name is Blake. I need to speak with you about your husband.\"\n\n---\n\nNow the confession scene. Blake is there to confess and to ask permission to bring Godric. This is a huge moment.\n\n---\n\nI let him in. What else could I do? He was Blake\u2014the Blake, the king's right hand, the man who had stood beside Godric since the day of the Awakening. Even if he had not been a figure of legend, I would have let him in, because there was something in his face that I recognized. Guilt. It was the same expression I wore when I looked at my husband and understood that all my efforts had failed.\n\nRogr came to the living room when I called him. He moved slowly, his hand trailing along the wall for support. When he saw Blake, he stopped. His face did not change\u2014it had lost the ability to change\u2014but his hands began to shake.\n\n\"I know you,\" Rogr said. It was the most present I had heard him sound in years.\n\nBlake nodded. \"You were in the delegation. Year 28.\"\n\n\"Yes.\"\n\nBlake sat down heavily on our worn sofa. He looked at his own hands for a long moment. Then he looked up at Rogr, and then at me, and then at Pete, who had come downstairs and was hovering in the doorway with wide, disbelieving eyes.\n\n\"I've come to apologize,\" Blake said. \"And to explain. And to ask your permission to make this right.\"\n\nThe story he told us was simple and monstrous. Ten years ago, before the delegation arrived, Blake had made an offhand comment to the king. A joke. A suggestion that Godric play into the tyrant persona that the newly subjugated peoples expected. Just for fun. Just to see their faces. Godric trusted Blake completely\u2014loved him, even\u2014and had taken the suggestion as a lark. He would pretend, for a moment, to be the cruel despot they feared. Then he would drop the act and reveal his true, warm nature. The prank would last a fraction of a second.\n\nBut Godric was Godric. Even an infinitesimal expression of displeasure\u2014a \u201cmild\u201d frown, held back by an amount so close to one hundred percent that the difference was a mathematical abstraction\u2014was not something a mortal mind could endure. The fraction of a second stretched into an eternity. The displeasure, however faint, however feigned, was a weight that no human soul was built to carry.\n\n\"The king thought it was nothing,\" Blake said, his voice cracking. \"He told me he held back nearly everything. He genuinely believed Rogr would recover. Mortal resilience, he called it. He was mortal once, a long time ago, and he... he forgot. He forgot what it means to be small.\"\n\n---\n\nGood. Now the family deliberates.\n\nBlake asks permission to bring Godric to heal Rogr. The family needs to discuss this.\n\n---\n\nThe silence that followed was a living thing. I looked at Rogr. Rogr looked at the floor. Pete looked at Blake with an expression that was equal parts awe and horror\u2014here was his hero's best friend, telling him that his hero had accidentally broken his father.\n\n\"I can bring him here,\" Blake said. \"The king. He can fix this. He can fix it instantly. But I won't do it without your permission. Any of you.\" He looked at me. \"Especially you.\"\n\n\"Why especially me?\" I asked.\n\n\"Because you've been the one fighting for him all these years,\" Blake said. \"Because you never gave up. Because what happened to Rogr happened on my suggestion, and I've carried that weight for ten years without even knowing it. Godric only mentioned it to me yesterday. Casually. As if it were a minor thing.\"\n\nCasually. My husband's shattered soul, a minor thing.\n\nI looked at Rogr. \"What do you want, love?\"\n\nRogr's mouth worked. For the first time in years, he seemed to be fighting for words instead of surrendering to silence. \"I want... I want to be able to look at you and feel what I used to feel. I want to hold your hand and not shake. I want to hear Pete talk about the king and not feel like I'm dying.\" A tear slid down his cheek. \"I'm so tired, Rosa. I'm so tired of being afraid.\"\n\n\"Then we say yes,\" I said.\n\nPete stepped forward. \"Does this mean the king is coming here? To our house?\"\n\nBlake nodded. \"The moment you agree.\"\n\n\"But we haven't agreed yet,\" I said. \"We need to\u2014\"\n\n\"We agree,\" Rogr said. The words came out strong, stronger than anything he had said in a decade. \"Tell him. Tell him we agree.\"\n\nAnd the moment the words left his mouth, the air in our living room changed. It did not shimmer or glow or crackle with power. It simply... accommodated. As if the universe had been holding its breath and had finally been given permission to exhale.\n\nGodric was standing by the window. He had not walked through the door or materialized in a flash of light. He was simply there, and he had always been there, and the part of my brain that understood causality took a long, slow blink and decided not to think about it too hard.\n\n---\n\nNow the healing scene. This needs to be terrifying in its ease:\n\n---\n\nHe looked exactly like his portraits. Forty-five, ruggedly handsome, with a salt-and-pepper beard trimmed neat and short hair that was more pepper than salt. He wore a simple tunic, nothing ostentatious. He could have been a retired soldier or a successful merchant. He could have been anyone.\n\nBut he was not anyone. He was Godric, and the moment I understood that I was in the presence of my king, my knees buckled. Not from fear. From awe. From the same instinct that makes a flower turn toward the sun.\n\n\"Please,\" Godric said, and his voice was exactly as warm as I had always imagined, \"don't kneel. This is your home.\"\n\nHe turned to Rogr. My husband, who had not stood straight in ten years, who had not met anyone's eyes in ten years, who had flinched at shadows and trembled at silences\u2014my husband looked at the king.\n\nAnd Godric looked back.\n\nI expected a ceremony. Words of power. A laying on of hands. Some visible sign that a miracle was occurring. But Godric simply stood there, his hands at his sides, his expression gentle. He did not move. He did not speak. He did not, as far as I could tell, do anything at all.\n\nRogr gasped.\n\nIt was a sharp, sudden inhalation, the kind a drowning man makes when his head breaks the surface. His spine straightened. His shoulders rolled back. The pallor that had haunted his skin for a decade flushed with healthy color. His eyes, those distant, haunted eyes, snapped into focus as if a lens had been adjusted behind them. The trembling in his hands\u2014that constant, fine vibration\u2014ceased entirely.\n\nAnd then he smiled. A real smile. The smile I had fallen in love with thirty years ago. The smile I had not seen since the morning he left for the delegation.\n\n\"Rosa,\" he said, and his voice was his voice, fully his voice, inflected with all the warmth and love and gentle humor I remembered. \"Rosa, I'm hungry. Can we have supper?\"\n\nI burst into tears. Ugly, heaving sobs that tore out of me with a force I could not control. I clutched Rogr\u2014my Rogr, my real Rogr\u2014and I felt his arms come around me, strong and sure and steady. He was solid. He was whole. He was healthy in a way that went beyond the mere absence of illness. He looked like a man who had never known a day of suffering in his life. His skin glowed. His eyes sparkled. His grip was firm and warm. He was the very picture of health, a vitality so complete it was almost otherworldly.\n\n---\n\nNow Rosa's complex emotions. The gratitude, the love, the dread.\n\n---\n\nI should have been nothing but grateful. And I was grateful. I was so grateful that the feeling felt like it might burn me up from the inside. I turned to thank the king, to fall at his feet and weep my thanks into the floorboards, but the words died in my throat.\n\nBecause I was thinking about the tincture. The tincture that had fled out the window. The memory-weaver whose fingertips had blackened. The chrono-therapist whose rods had melted. The dream-catcher that had screamed in the garden. Ten years of effort. Ten years of hope and failure and despair and stubborn, desperate love. Ten years of watching my husband wither while I threw everything I had at an invisible wall.\n\nAnd the king had fixed it by deciding it should be fixed. He had not moved. He had not spoken. He had simply... decided. The ease of it was a chasm opening beneath my feet. If the cure was that effortless, then what had been the cause? What kind of power did you have to possess for a fraction of a second's mild displeasure to do what it had done to Rogr? What kind of being could break a man so thoroughly and heal him so casually?\n\nI looked at Godric, who was smiling at Rogr with genuine warmth, and I felt two things at once: a love so immense it could crush me, and a dread so deep it had no bottom.\n\nHe turned to me. His eyes met mine. And I knew, with absolute certainty, that he knew exactly what I was feeling. Both things. All of them. He knew about the tinctures and the healers and the nights I had spent crying on the bathroom floor. He had always known. He had known from the very first second.\n\n\"I owe you a debt,\" Godric said. His voice was soft. \"You fought for your husband with everything you had. I underestimated the harm my prank caused, and I relied too much on the resilience I once knew as a mortal. You suffered for my misjudgment. Name any compensation, and it is yours.\"\n\nThe offer was not a casual thing. I could feel the weight of it. He meant it. I could ask for anything\u2014wealth beyond measure, power beyond imagination, a place in the court, immortality itself. I could ask for a galaxy and he would give it to me with the same ease he had healed my husband.\n\nI shook my head. My voice came out steadier than I expected. \"You've already given me what I wanted. I don't need anything else.\"\n\nHe studied me for a moment. Then he nodded, a simple, respectful acknowledgment. \"As you wish.\"\n\nNo argument. No insistence. No dramatic declarations about my humility. He accepted my refusal with the same effortless grace he had used to cure Rogr, and somehow, that was the most terrifying thing of all.\n\n---\n\nNow Pete's reaction, and the family's continued reverence:\n\n---\n\nPete was on his knees. My son, who had grown up worshipping the king from afar, was on his knees in our living room, his face wet with tears. \"Thank you,\" he kept saying. \"Thank you, thank you, thank you.\"\n\nGodric reached down and gently pulled Pete to his feet. \"Your father is a strong man,\" he said. \"He endured something no one should have endured. Be proud of him.\"\n\nPete nodded, unable to speak. Rogr\u2014whole, healthy, radiant Rogr\u2014put his arm around our son's shoulders and pulled him close. I watched my family, reunited in a way I had given up hope of ever seeing, and I felt joy so sharp it cut.\n\nAnd underneath the joy, the dread remained. A thin, persistent hum at the base of my skull. It whispered that the king who stood in my living room, the king who had healed my husband with a thought, the king whose warmth was so genuine and whose smile was so kind\u2014that same king could unmake everything I loved without even noticing he had done it. Not because he was cruel. Because he was so far beyond us that our entire existence was a rounding error in the mathematics of his being.\n\nI loved him. Everyone in that room loved him. Rogr, who had been broken by him, looked at him with an adoration that bordered on religious ecstasy. Pete was still trembling with the honor of being touched by the king's hand. And I\u2014I loved him too. Fiercely. Genuinely. The love was not pretend. It was the truest thing I felt.\n\nBut so was the dread.\n\n---\n\nNow the ending with Godric joking to Blake:\n\n---\n\nAs the king prepared to leave\u2014or rather, as he decided to stop being present\u2014he paused beside Blake. His expression was playful, the kind of look a friend gives another friend when they're about to share a private joke.\n\n\"That went rather well,\" Godric said, his voice pitched low enough that only Blake and those of us nearby could hear. \"We should plan another prank sometime. Something a bit more elaborate, perhaps.\"\n\nBlake's face went white. \"Absolutely not,\" he said, and his voice was a blade. \"Never again. Do you understand me? Never again.\"\n\nGodric raised an eyebrow. His smile did not waver. \"It was a joke, my friend.\"\n\n\"The last one was a joke too,\" Blake said. \"And it destroyed a man for ten years.\"\n\nGodric's smile faded. Not into anger\u2014into thoughtfulness. \"You're right,\" he said. \"Of course you're right.\" He looked at Rogr, then back at Blake. \"No more pranks.\"\n\nBut as he vanished\u2014not fading, not teleporting, simply ceasing to occupy the space he had occupied\u2014I caught something in his expression that made the dread hum louder. It was not malice. It was not even carelessness. It was the look of a being who was trying, genuinely trying, to remember what it felt like to be fragile. And failing.\n\nBlake stayed behind for a moment. He looked at me, and his eyes were full of an apology that words could not contain. \"I'm sorry,\" he said. \"For all of it.\"\n\n\"He's healed now,\" I said. \"That's what matters.\"\n\nBut as I spoke the words, I could hear the dream-catcher's muffled whimper rising from the garden, and I knew that some things, once buried, never truly stopped screaming.\n\n---\n\nOK, this is roughly 3000-3500 words.                     "text": "The tincture boiled over the rim of the copper pot, each bubble popping with a sound like a tiny, swallowed scream. I snatched the vessel from the flame, my fingers wrapped in three layers of cloth, and tipped the liquid toward the catch-basin. One drop escaped, touched the wooden table, and the wood puckered. Not burned\u2014flinched. The drop shivered, gathered itself, and shot upward, out the open window, leaving behind a faint trail of ozone and the smell of something that had been very, very afraid.\n\nI had spent six months acquiring that tincture. Six months traveling to the outer markets, bartering my grandmother's silver combs and three bolts of Thronegate silk. The Ythrian merchant with oil-slick eyes had sworn it could pull trauma from the marrow like a splinter from a thumb. He had demonstrated on a wounded animal\u2014a small, trembling creature with matted fur\u2014and I had watched the tension drain from its body as the liquid touched its tongue. Now the tincture had fled. Not evaporated. Fled.\n\n\"Rosa.\" Rogr's voice from the bedroom. Thin. Even. The voice of a man who had forgotten how to inflect.\n\n\"Coming,\" I called, and I manufactured brightness the way a factory manufactures sheet metal. I had learned to make my voice bright. It was a survival mechanism, like the way your hand learns to pull back from a hot iron, except the hot iron was my husband's silence and I could not pull away from it.\n\nI found him in the armchair by the window, the one that caught the afternoon light. He sat in the light but did not seem to feel it. His hands lay on his thighs, palms up, fingers slightly curled. Ten years ago, those hands had been a carpenter's hands\u2014calloused, sure, always moving. They had built the table the tincture had just rejected. They had carved Pete's crib, sanded it smooth enough for an infant's cheek. Now they were still. They were always still.\n\nI knelt beside him and took one of those hands. The skin was cool and dry, the texture of paper that had been left in the sun too long. \"I made you some tea,\" I said. \"The blend from the eastern provinces. You liked it last winter.\"\n\n\"That's nice,\" he said, and his eyes did not move from the window. Outside, the spires of Thronegate City glittered in the midday sun. The king's banner, silver on black, snapped in a wind I could not feel. A parade was coming. The twentieth year of the Awakening. Banners were being hung on every street corner, and the children were practicing their songs.\n\n\"I saw the decorations going up on Harrow Street,\" I said, because silence with Rogr was a living thing that grew if you let it. \"Mrs. Calloway has strung lanterns all the way from her bakery to the fountain. She said the king might walk that way after the ceremony.\"\n\nAt the mention of the king, Rogr's hand twitched in mine. A spasm so brief I might have imagined it. But I had learned to see these things. I had ten years of practice.\n\n\"Pete wants to go,\" I continued, keeping my voice steady. \"To the parade. He's been talking about it for a month. He wants to see the king.\"\n\n\"Then he should go,\" Rogr said.\n\n\"We should all go,\" I said. \"As a family.\"\n\nRogr's hand twitched again. \"I don't... crowds are...\" He stopped. He often stopped mid-sentence, as if the words had been stolen from his mouth by a thief only he could see. His jaw worked. A muscle in his cheek jumped. Then his face smoothed back into that terrible, placid emptiness, and he said nothing more.\n\nI stayed kneeling beside him until my knees ached, and then I stayed longer.\n\n---\n\nI remember the day he came back. Year 28. The delegation had been a great honor\u2014newly subjugated universes sending representatives to pledge fealty to the king for the first time. A historic moment. Rogr had been selected as a cultural liaison, a role that recognized his years of service in the merchant guild. He had left our home on a Tuesday morning, the spring light catching the gray at his temples, and he had kissed me on the forehead and told me he would be home for supper with stories to tell.\n\nHe was not home for supper. He came home three days later, his clothes unchanged, his face a ruin of something I could not name. I had been frantic. I had contacted the guild, the city guard, the palace liaison office. No one knew anything. No one could tell me where my husband was.\n\nWhen he finally walked through the door, I threw myself at him, my hands checking him for wounds, for blood, for any physical sign of what had happened. There were no wounds. There was no blood. There was only the look in his eyes, which was the look of a man who had seen something that did not fit inside a human skull.\n\n\"What happened?\" I had asked, pulling him inside, guiding him to the sofa. \"Rogr, please, you're scaring me. What happened?\"\n\n\"Nothing,\" he had said. \"Nothing happened. It was... the king. I met the king.\"\n\nThe king. Godric the Eternal. The benevolent ruler who had given us the Awakening, who had made Thronegate the center of the omniverse, who ensured that no woman walked in fear and no child went hungry. The king whose portrait hung in every public square, whose voice on the broadcasts was warm and paternal. The king I loved with the uncomplicated devotion of a subject who had never known anything but peace.\n\n\"What about the king?\" I had pressed. \"Rogr, what did he do?\"\n\nRogr had opened his mouth. And then he had closed it. And then he had started to shake\u2014a fine, rapid trembling that began in his fingers and spread inward, until his entire body was quaking like a plucked string. I had wrapped him in blankets and held him until the shaking stopped, and when it stopped, something in him had stopped with it. Something I could not name and could not reach and could not heal.\n\nHe never told me what the king had done. He could not. The words did not exist. Over the years, I pieced together fragments\u2014a look, a moment of displeasure, a weight that pressed down on him from every direction at once\u2014but the fragments never cohered into a picture I could understand. How could a look break a man? How could a moment's displeasure, held back by the king's own restraint, shatter something so fundamental that it could not be reassembled?\n\nI did not understand. And because I did not understand, I could not help. And because I could not help, I watched my husband die by inches while his heart stubbornly continued to beat.\n\n---\n\nThe king was good. Everyone knew the king was good. Under his rule, Thronegate had become a paradise\u2014streets clean of crime, markets overflowing with goods from a thousand universes, children growing up with strong bones and straight teeth. The Awakening had made our men mighty, and that might was used only to protect, never to oppress. When I walked through the city, I saw women smiling without the tightness around their eyes that my grandmother had described. I saw old men playing chess in the parks without fear of being robbed. I saw young couples holding hands without looking over their shoulders. This was the world Godric had built, and I was grateful for it with every fiber of my being.\n\nAnd my husband had been broken by it. By him. The contradiction was a splinter in my mind that I could not work loose. Every time I saw a mother lift her laughing child toward the king's portrait so the child could wave, I felt my heart split along a seam I had not known was there. Every time a neighbor praised the king's justice, the king's generosity, the king's unwavering protection of Thronegate's citizens, I nodded and smiled and felt the splinter dig deeper.\n\nMy son Pete adored the king with the uncomplicated fervor of the young. He collected the commemorative coins minted for each year of Godric's reign, arranging them in a velvet-lined box he had saved three months to buy. He could recite the names of the five Thronegate men who had conquered Elaris\u2014Hal the baker, Joren the cobbler, Mikkel the scribe, Tamas the mason, and Wendel the gardener\u2014as easily as other boys recited sports statistics. When he spoke of the king, his voice grew hushed and reverent, as if he were speaking of a household god who happened to sit on a throne.\n\n\"Father met him,\" Pete would say to his friends, his chest puffing with pride. \"Father was in the delegation. He stood in the same room as the king. He breathed the same air.\"\n\nAnd Rogr, whenever he overheard this, would turn his face to the wall and say nothing. I would watch the light die in his eyes a little more each time, and I would want to take Pete by the shoulders and shake him and tell him to stop, stop reminding his father of the thing that had ruined him. But I could not. Because Pete was proud of his father. Because Pete loved the king. Because Pete was a child of Thronegate, and Thronegate children loved their king, and that love was beautiful and good and true.\n\nThe splinter dug deeper.\n\n---\n\nIn Year 30, two years after the delegation, I saved enough credit to hire a memory-weaver from the Hathorian Collective. She arrived in robes of spun silver, her fingers long and delicate as spider legs, her face serene with the confidence of someone who had healed ten thousand troubled minds. She claimed she could enter a patient's memories and smooth the jagged edges of traumatic recollection, like sanding down a splintered board.\n\nShe placed her fingertips on Rogr's temples, closed her eyes, and began to hum. The hum was a soothing thing, a lullaby from a distant star, and for a moment, I allowed myself to hope. And then she screamed.\n\nThe sound was not a sound so much as a physical extrusion\u2014a spike of pure, undiluted terror that shattered every glass object in our home. The windows cracked. The mirrors splintered. A vase my mother had given me on my wedding day exploded into a thousand shards. The memory-weaver wrenched her hands away from Rogr's head, and I saw that her fingertips were blackened, the skin shriveled and cracked as if she had pressed them against the surface of a star.\n\nShe fled our house without speaking. She did not stop to collect her fee. She did not stop to gather her equipment. She simply ran, her silver robes streaming behind her, and the next day we heard she had booked passage to the farthest edge of the known omniverse and refused to ever speak of what she had seen inside my husband's mind.\n\nRogr apologized to me. He sat in his armchair, his hands trembling, and he said, \"I'm sorry, Rosa. I didn't mean for that to happen.\"\n\nI held him and told him it was not his fault, and I believed it, but I also felt something cold and heavy settle in my stomach, something that whispered that whatever had been done to my husband was beyond the reach of any healer I could afford.\n\n---\n\nIn Year 33, I found a chrono-therapist who operated out of a converted observatory in the lower districts. His name was Kellan, and he claimed he could unwind time around a specific memory, diluting its impact by spreading it across multiple timelines. He had testimonials. He had certificates. He had a waiting room full of patients who swore he had given them their lives back.\n\nHe set up his equipment in our living room\u2014a series of crystalline rods that hummed with temporal energy, their facets catching the light and scattering it into rainbows that danced across the walls. He calibrated the rods, adjusting each one with the care of a musician tuning an instrument. He aimed them at Rogr, who sat in his armchair with his hands on his knees and his eyes fixed on nothing.\n\n\"Now,\" Kellan said, \"you may feel a slight pulling sensation. That's perfectly normal. It's just the memory being stretched across the temporal lattice.\"\n\nHe activated the device.\n\nThe rods melted. Not from heat\u2014there was no heat. They melted from sheer ontological rejection, as if the very concept of their function had been declared invalid by some higher authority. The crystalline structure did not crack or shatter; it simply lost cohesion, slumping into puddles of inert slag that dripped onto my floor and ate holes through the wood. Kellan stared at the destruction and said, very quietly, \"There is nothing wrong with your husband that I can fix.\"\n\nHe packed his surviving equipment and left without charging a fee. On his way out, he paused at the door and looked back at me. His face was pale. His hands were shaking. \"I don't know what happened to him,\" he said. \"But you should stop trying to fix it. You should just... stop.\" Then he was gone, and the holes in my floor remained, and the smell of molten crystal hung in the air for weeks.\n\n---\n\nBy Year 36, I was desperate in a way that had ceased to feel like desperation and had become simply the texture of my days. I stopped sleeping through the night. I stopped eating meals at regular hours. My body became a machine for caring for Rogr and searching for cures, and I fed it just enough to keep it running.\n\nThe dream-catcher was my last true hope. I bought it from a vendor in the shadow markets\u2014a place I had never imagined I would enter, a place where the goods were strange and the merchants spoke in half-truths and the currency was not always money. It was woven from the silk of chrono-spiders, creatures that spun webs across the axis of time itself. The vendor, a woman with too many teeth, told me it would filter nightmares, catch the dark thoughts before they could sink their hooks into the sleeping mind. She told me it had never failed.\n\nI hung it above Rogr's bed with my own hands, looping the silk thread around a hook I had screwed into the ceiling. For one night, it held. I know it held, because Rogr slept without waking, without the small, wounded sounds he usually made in his sleep. I lay beside him and listened to the silence and felt hope bloom in my chest like a flower opening toward the sun.\n\nIn the morning, the dream-catcher had unraveled. The threads had recoiled into a tight, terrified knot no larger than a walnut, pulling so far into themselves that the hook had been ripped from the ceiling and lay on the pillow beside Rogr's head. When I reached for the knot, it emitted a sound\u2014a high, keening whimper that went on and on, a sound of pure, abject fear. I carried it to the garden and buried it under three feet of soil, and for days afterward I could hear it crying, a muffled, hopeless wail rising through the earth like the voice of something that had looked into an abyss and found the abyss looking back.\n\nI stopped trying after that. Not because I had given up on Rogr. Never that. But because I had run out of things to try. I had scoured the known omniverse for remedies, spent our savings, sold my grandmother's jewelry, begged favors from people who did not grant favors lightly. And every attempt had failed not just uselessly but catastrophically, each cure recoiling from my husband's condition as if from a predator.\n\nBy Year 38, Rogr had stopped leaving the bedroom. He ate when I brought him food, but he no longer tasted it. His carpenter's hands, once so strong, had grown thin and translucent. I could see the veins beneath his skin, blue rivers mapping a country I could not visit. Pete had stopped bringing friends over. He still loved the king\u2014that love was too deeply rooted to be dislodged\u2014but he had learned to lower his voice, to move carefully, to live in a house that had become a sickroom.\n\n---\n\nI was in the kitchen washing dishes on a Thursday afternoon when the knock came at the door. Not a servant's knock. Not a neighbor's. This knock was firm, deliberate, and accompanied by a presence that made the air in the hallway thicken, as if the house itself was holding its breath.\n\nI opened the door. The man standing on my threshold was old\u2014seventy-four, I would later learn\u2014but his shoulders were straight and his eyes were sharp. He had a friendly mutton chop, the kind of facial hair that made a man look like someone's favorite uncle. He wore the robes of a court wizard, the silver-and-blue fabric rippling with patterns that seemed to shift when I looked at them directly. On his collar was embroidered a symbol I did not recognize\u2014a circle bisected by a zigzag line, like a bolt of lightning frozen in thread. When he lifted his hand in greeting, his fingers traced a small, automatic gesture in the air, something that felt older than the city, older than Thronegate itself. Then his hand dropped, and the moment passed, and he was just an old man on my doorstep.\n\n\"Rosa,\" he said, and his voice was heavy with something that sounded like grief. \"My name is Blake. I need to speak with you about your husband.\"\n\nI let him in. What else could I do? He was Blake\u2014the Blake, the king's right hand, the man who had stood beside Godric since the day of the Awakening. Even if he had not been a figure of legend, I would have let him in, because there was something in his face that I recognized. Guilt. It was the same expression I wore when I looked at my husband and understood that all my efforts had failed.\n\nRogr came to the living room when I called him. He moved slowly, his hand trailing along the wall for support. When he saw Blake, he stopped. His face did not change\u2014it had lost the ability to change\u2014but his hands began to shake with that familiar, terrible trembling.\n\n\"I know you,\" Rogr said. It was the most present I had heard him sound in years.\n\nBlake nodded. \"You were in the delegation. Year 28. You and thirty-seven others.\"\n\n\"Yes.\"\n\nBlake sat down heavily on our worn sofa. He looked at his own hands for a long moment\u2014hands that were calloused in a way I had not expected, hands that looked like they had done honest work once. Then he looked up at Rogr, and then at me, and then at Pete, who had come downstairs and was hovering in the doorway with wide, disbelieving eyes, his mouth slightly open.\n\n\"I've come to apologize,\" Blake said. \"And to explain. And to ask your permission to make this right.\"\n\nThe story he told us was simple and monstrous. Ten years ago, before the delegation arrived, Blake had made an offhand comment to the king. A joke. A suggestion that Godric play into the tyrant persona that the newly subjugated peoples expected. Just for fun. Just to see their faces. The king trusted Blake completely\u2014loved him, even\u2014and had taken the suggestion as a lark. He would pretend, for a fraction of a moment, to be the cruel despot they feared. Then he would drop the act and reveal his true, warm nature. The prank would last no time at all, barely a heartbeat.\n\nBut Godric was Godric. Even an infinitesimal expression of displeasure\u2014a \"mild\" frown, held back by an amount so close to one hundred percent that the difference was a mathematical abstraction\u2014was not something a mortal mind could endure. The fraction of a second stretched into an eternity for the delegates who caught the edge of it. The displeasure, however faint, however feigned, was a weight that no human soul was built to carry.\n\nBlake's voice cracked. \"The king thought it was nothing. He told me he held back nearly everything. He genuinely believed your husband would recover. Mortal resilience, he called it. He said he was mortal once, a long time ago, and he believed in the strength of mortal men to endure hardship. He...\" Blake stopped. Swallowed. \"He forgot what it means to be small.\"\n\nI stared at him. The splinter in my mind twisted. \"You're telling me,\" I said, and my voice came out very calm, \"that my husband has been dying in front of me for ten years because of a practical joke?\"\n\n\"I only learned of it yesterday,\" Blake said. \"Godric mentioned it casually. Offhandedly. As if it were a minor thing that had slipped his mind. I shouted at him. I shouted at the king. I told him he should have told me, that he had no right to keep this from me, that he had destroyed a man and then forgotten about it.\" Blake's hands were shaking now, matching Rogr's tremor for tremor. \"He didn't understand why I was angry. He still doesn't fully understand. He thinks it was a small thing. He doesn't... he can't...\"\n\nHe trailed off. The silence stretched.\n\n\"I can bring him here,\" Blake said finally. \"The king. He can fix this. He can fix it instantly. It would take no effort. He could have done it at any moment during these past ten years, but he didn't, because he thought it wasn't serious, because he believed Rogr would recover on his own, because he has forgotten what it means to be hurt by something you can't fight back against.\" Blake's voice was raw now, scraped clean of diplomacy. \"I'm asking your permission. All of you. I won't let him come unless you want him to.\"\n\nThe silence that followed was deeper than any silence I had known. It was the silence of a decade collapsing into a single question.\n\nI looked at Rogr. \"What do you want, love?\"\n\nRogr's mouth worked. His jaw clenched and unclenched. A sound came out of him, low and broken, and I realized he was trying to speak and could not. He had forgotten how to ask for help. He had forgotten that help was even possible. I watched him fight for the words with the same desperate, stubborn strength I had watched him fight for everything else over the past ten years.\n\n\"Father,\" Pete said, stepping forward. His voice was high and tight. \"Please. Please let the king come. Please let him fix it.\"\n\nRogr looked at our son. Something in his face shifted\u2014a crack in the wall that had been built around him. \"I want...\" He swallowed. \"I want to hold your mother's hand and not shake. I want to hear you talk about the king and not feel like I'm dying. I want to walk outside and see the sun and not feel afraid of the light.\" Tears were running down his cheeks, and he did not wipe them away. \"I'm so tired, Rosa. I'm so tired of being broken.\"\n\n\"Then we say yes,\" I said.\n\nPete grabbed my arm. \"The king is coming here? To our house?\"\n\nBlake nodded. \"The moment you agree.\"\n\n\"But we haven't\u2014\" I started.\n\n\"We agree,\" Rogr said. The words came out strong, stronger than anything he had said in ten years. It was the voice of the man I had married, the man who had once carried me across the threshold of this house and promised me a lifetime of happiness. \"Tell him.\"\n\nBlake closed his eyes. He did not speak. He did not send a message or make a gesture or activate any device. He simply closed his eyes, and the moment he did, the air in our living room changed. It did not shimmer or glow or crackle with power. It simply accommodated. As if the universe had been holding its breath and had finally been given permission to exhale.\n\nGodric was standing by the window. He had not walked through the door. He had not materialized in a flash of light. He was simply there, and he had always been there, and the part of my brain that understood causality took a long, slow blink and decided not to think about it too hard.\n\nHe looked exactly like his portraits and nothing like his portraits. The portraits captured his features\u2014the rugged handsomeness, the salt-and-pepper beard trimmed neat, the short hair that was more pepper than salt, the muscular build that spoke of a man in his prime\u2014but they could not capture the presence. The presence was not something I could see or hear or smell. It was something I felt in my bones, a deep, resonant recognition that I was in the presence of something that could unmake me without effort and had chosen not to. The portraits made him look like a king. The reality made him feel like the concept of kingship given flesh.\n\nHe wore a simple tunic, gray fabric that looked soft to the touch. His hands were at his sides. His expression was gentle. He could have been a retired soldier or a successful merchant. He could have been anyone.\n\n\"Please,\" he said, and his voice was exactly as warm as I had always imagined, \"don't kneel. This is your home. I'm a guest here.\"\n\nMy knees had not buckled. I had not knelt. But I had wanted to. Every cell in my body had wanted to lower itself before him, and I only realized I had resisted when the urge passed and left me trembling.\n\nGodric turned to Rogr. My husband, who had not stood straight in ten years, who had not met anyone's eyes in ten years, who had flinched at shadows and trembled at silences\u2014my husband looked at the king. And the king looked back.\n\nI expected a ceremony. Words of power spoken in a language that predated stars. A laying on of hands, the king's palms pressed to my husband's temples. Some visible sign that a miracle was being performed. But Godric simply stood there, his hands at his sides, his expression gentle and slightly sad. He did not move. He did not speak. He did not, as far as I could tell, do anything at all.\n\nRogr gasped.\n\nIt was a sharp, sudden inhalation, the kind a drowning man makes when his head breaks the surface after too long underwater. His spine straightened, vertebra by vertebra, uncurling from the stoop that had become his default posture. His shoulders rolled back, filling the shirt that had hung loose on his frame for years. The pallor that had haunted his skin flushed with healthy color, pink rising in his cheeks and his throat and his hands. His eyes\u2014those distant, haunted, empty eyes\u2014snapped into focus as if a lens had been adjusted behind them, the pupils contracting, the irises brightening, the whites clearing of the yellow film that had crept across them.\n\nAnd then he smiled. A real smile. The smile I had fallen in love with thirty years ago, that crooked tilt of the mouth that had made my heart stutter the first time I saw it. The smile I had not witnessed since the morning he left for the delegation.\n\n\"Rosa,\" he said, and his voice was his voice, fully his voice, inflected with all the warmth and love and gentle humor I remembered from the years before the shadow fell over him. \"Rosa, I'm hungry. Can we have supper?\"\n\nI burst into tears.\n\nUgly, heaving sobs that tore out of me with a force I could not control, sounds that were less like crying and more like being sick, my body purging ten years of grief in a single convulsive release. I clutched Rogr\u2014my Rogr, my real Rogr, the man I had married and loved and fought for and nearly lost\u2014and I felt his arms come around me, strong and sure and steady. No trembling. No hesitation. Just the solid, warm, living weight of my husband holding me up.\n\nHe was whole. He was healthy in a way that went beyond the mere absence of illness. His skin glowed with a vitality that was almost luminous, as if someone had lit a candle inside his chest. His grip was firm and warm. The lines on his face had not vanished\u2014he was not younger, not de-aged\u2014but they had transformed from the creases of a man being slowly crushed into the laugh lines of a man who had lived well. He was the very picture of health, a vibrancy so complete it was almost otherworldly. He looked like a man who had never known a day of suffering. He looked like a man who had been handcrafted by a benevolent god as a gift to the world.\n\nI should have been nothing but grateful. And I was grateful. I was so grateful that the feeling felt like it might burn me up from the inside, like my body was too small to contain the magnitude of my relief. I turned to thank the king, to fall at his feet and weep my thanks into the floorboards, but the words died in my throat before they could form.\n\nBecause I was thinking about the tincture. The tincture that had fled out the window like a frightened animal. The memory-weaver whose fingertips had blackened and who had fled to the edge of the omniverse. The chrono-therapist whose rods had melted into slag. The dream-catcher that had screamed in the garden for days after I buried it. Ten years of effort. Ten years of hope and failure and despair and stubborn, desperate love. Ten years of watching my husband wither while I threw everything I had at an invisible wall.\n\nAnd the king had fixed it by deciding it should be fixed. He had not moved. He had not spoken. He had not exerted himself in any way I could perceive. He had simply... decided. The ease of it was a chasm opening beneath my feet, and I was falling into it and could not stop falling.\n\nIf the cure was that effortless\u2014if my husband's shattered soul could be made whole by a thought that cost the king less than a breath\u2014then what had been the cause? What kind of power did you have to possess for a fraction of a second's mild displeasure to do what it had done to Rogr? What kind of being could break a man so thoroughly and heal him so casually, and not understand the difference between the two?\n\nI looked at Godric, who was smiling at Rogr with genuine warmth in his eyes, and I felt three things at once, each one as true as the others. Love. Gratitude. And a dread so deep it had no bottom.\n\nHe turned to me. His eyes met mine\u2014gray eyes, kind eyes, eyes that had seen the birth and death of universes and the rise and fall of civilizations beyond counting. And I knew, with absolute certainty, that he knew exactly what I was feeling. All of it. The love. The gratitude. The dread. He knew about the tinctures and the healers and the nights I had spent crying on the bathroom floor so Rogr would not hear me. He had always known. He had known from the very first second the shadow fell over my husband.\n\n\"I owe you a debt,\" Godric said. His voice was soft, pitched for me alone even though the room was silent. \"You fought for your husband with everything you had. I underestimated the harm my actions caused. I held back so much of myself that I thought the remainder was trivial. I was wrong. You suffered for ten years because of my misjudgment, and I am sorry.\" He paused. \"Name any compensation, and it is yours. Anything within the omniverse. Anything beyond it. It is yours.\"\n\nThe offer was not a casual thing. I could feel the weight of it pressing against the air, making the walls seem thinner and the floor less solid. He meant it. Every word. I could ask for anything\u2014wealth beyond the GDP of a thousand planets, power beyond the reach of any mortal institution, a place in the court, immortality itself. I could ask for a galaxy, a universe, a throne of my own amid the stars. Whatever I named, he would give it to me with the same effortless decision that had healed my husband. It would cost him nothing. It would mean nothing to him. It would be less than a rounding error in the mathematics of his existence.\n\nI shook my head. My voice came out steadier than I expected, though my hands were trembling. \"You've already given me what I wanted. For ten years, the only thing I wanted was my husband back. I don't need anything else.\"\n\nHe studied me for a long moment. The gray eyes held mine, and I felt seen in a way I had never been seen before\u2014every thought, every fear, every desperate prayer I had whispered into the darkness of my bedroom when I thought no one was listening. He saw it all. And he did not flinch from it.\n\n\"As you wish,\" he said.\n\nNo argument. No insistence. No dramatic declarations about my humility or my virtue. He accepted my refusal with the same effortless grace he had used to cure Rogr, the same nonchalance that had characterized the entire exchange. And somehow, that was the most terrifying thing of all. The ease. The absolute, unshakeable ease with which he moved through the world, healing and harming with the same casual omnipotence, accepting my refusal of astronomical wealth as if I had declined a second cup of tea.\n\nPete was on his knees. My son, who had grown up worshipping the king from afar, who had collected coins and memorized stories and defended Godric's name against any whisper of criticism\u2014my son was on his knees in our living room, his face wet with tears, his hands clasped in front of him like a supplicant at an altar.\n\n\"Thank you,\" Pete kept saying, the words tumbling over each other. \"Thank you, thank you, thank you. I knew you would save him. I knew it. I've always known you were good. Everyone said you were good, and I told them, I told them you were. Thank you. Thank you.\"\n\nGodric reached down and gently pulled Pete to his feet. The king's hands\u2014hands that could erase galaxies\u2014rested on my son's shoulders with a tenderness that made my chest ache.\n\n\"Your father is a strong man,\" Godric said. \"He endured something no one should have endured. Be proud of him.\"\n\nPete nodded, unable to speak. Rogr\u2014whole, healthy, radiant Rogr\u2014put his arm around our son's shoulders and pulled him close. His other arm was still around me. The three of us stood together in the living room of our small house, the king before us and the afternoon light falling through the window, and for a moment I let myself believe that the nightmare was over.\n\nBut the dread did not leave. It settled into the base of my skull, a thin, persistent hum like the sound of a distant engine that never stopped running. It whispered that the king who stood in my living room, the king who had healed my husband with a thought, the king whose warmth was so genuine and whose smile was so kind\u2014that same king could unmake everything I loved without even noticing he had done it. Not because he was cruel. He was not cruel. He was something far more terrifying: a being for whom the distinction between breaking and healing was so fine that he could not always perceive it.\n\nI loved him. Everyone in that room loved him. Rogr, who had been broken by him, looked at him with an adoration that bordered on religious ecstasy, his whole body oriented toward the king like a flower toward the sun. Pete was still trembling from the honor of being touched by the royal hand. And I\u2014I loved him too. Fiercely. Genuinely. The love was not a mask or a performance or a submission to inevitability. It was the truest thing I felt.\n\nBut so was the dread. The two things existed side by side in my heart, and I did not know how to reconcile them, and I suspected I never would.\n\nAs the king prepared to leave\u2014or rather, as he decided to stop being present in our home\u2014he paused beside Blake. His expression shifted, the solemnity giving way to something lighter, something almost mischievous. The look of a friend sharing a private joke.\n\n\"That went rather well,\" Godric said, his voice pitched low enough that only Blake and those of us nearby could hear. \"We should plan another prank sometime. Something a bit more elaborate, perhaps. The delegates from the Ythrian Collective are arriving next month\u2014I hear they're delightfully pompous.\"\n\nBlake's face went white. The color drained from his cheeks so fast I thought he might collapse. \"Absolutely not,\" he said, and his voice was a blade drawn across silk. \"Never again. Do you understand me? Never. Again.\"\n\nGodric raised an eyebrow. His smile did not waver, but something in his eyes flickered\u2014not anger, not offense, but a kind of mild puzzlement, as if he were trying to solve a riddle that did not quite make sense. \"It was a joke, my friend.\"\n\n\"The last one was a joke too,\" Blake said. \"And it destroyed a man for ten years. Ten years, Godric. I only found out yesterday because you mentioned it in passing, like it was a bit of interesting trivia. You have absolute omniscience. You knew. You knew the entire time, and you didn't think it was worth mentioning.\"\n\nGodric's smile faded. Not into anger\u2014into thoughtfulness. The same expression I had seen on my own face in the mirror a thousand times, when I realized I had forgotten something important and was trying to calculate the damage. \"You're right,\" he said after a moment. \"Of course you're right. I apologize, Blake. I will... endeavor to be more mindful.\" He glanced at Rogr, then back at Blake. \"No more pranks.\"\n\nHis voice was sincere. I believed him. In that moment, I believed that the king meant every word, that he genuinely regretted the harm he had caused, that he would try his best to prevent it from happening again.\n\nBut as he vanished\u2014not fading, not teleporting, not dissolving into light, simply ceasing to occupy the space he had occupied\u2014I caught something in his expression that made the dread hum louder. It was not malice. It was not even carelessness. It was the look of a being who was trying, genuinely and sincerely trying, to remember what it felt like to be fragile. And failing. The look of a man who had once been mortal and could no longer access that mortality, like a language he had spoken fluently as a child and had since forgotten every word of.\n\nBlake stayed behind for a moment. He looked at me, and his eyes were full of an apology that words could not contain. The folds of his face seemed deeper than they had been, the lines around his mouth carved by more than just age. \"I'm sorry,\" he said. \"For all of it. For the prank. For not knowing. For not coming sooner.\"\n\n\"He's healed now,\" I said. The words came out automatically, a reflex. \"That's what matters.\"\n\nAnd it was what mattered. My husband was whole. My son had his father back. Our family had been restored to us by the grace of a king who had meant no harm and had caused it anyway.\n\nBut as I spoke the words, I could almost hear the dream-catcher's muffled whimper rising from the garden, could almost feel the tincture fleeing from its own container, could almost see the memory-weaver's blackened fingertips and the chrono-therapist's melted rods. Some things, once broken, did not unbreak. Some fears, once planted, continued to grow in the dark soil of the mind.\n\nRogr took my hand. His grip was firm and warm and steady. He was smiling. Pete was laughing, actually laughing, the sound bright and startled and joyful. My family was whole. My king was good. My world was exactly as it should be.\n\nThe dread hummed on."
